</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Discrimination_sidebar" title="Template:Discrimination sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Discrimination_sidebar" title="Template talk:Discrimination sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Discrimination_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Racism</b> is the belief that groups of humans possess different behavioral traits corresponding to inherited attributes and can be divided based on the superiority of one <a href="/wiki/Race_(human_categorization)" title="Race (human categorization)">race</a> over another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dennis_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dennis-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghani_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ghani-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newman,_D.M._3-0"><a href="#cite_note-newman,_D.M.-3">[3]</a></sup> It may also mean <a href="/wiki/Prejudice" title="Prejudice">prejudice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discrimination</a>, or antagonism directed against other people because they are of a different race or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnicity" title="Ethnicity">ethnicity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghani_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ghani-2">[2]</a></sup> Modern variants of racism are often based in social perceptions of biological differences between peoples. These views can take the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_actions" title="Social actions">social actions</a>, practices or beliefs, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_systems" title="Political systems">political systems</a> in which different races are ranked as inherently superior or inferior to each other, based on presumed shared inheritable traits, abilities, or qualities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghani_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ghani-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newman_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-newman-4">[4]</a></sup> There have been attempts to legitimize racist beliefs through scientific means, such as <a href="/wiki/Scientific_racism" title="Scientific racism">scientific racism</a>, which have been overwhelmingly shown to be unfounded. In terms of political systems (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a>) that support the expression of prejudice or aversion in discriminatory practices or laws, racist ideology may include associated social aspects such as <a href="/wiki/Nativism_(politics)" title="Nativism (politics)">nativism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia" title="Xenophobia">xenophobia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Other_(philosophy)" title="Other (philosophy)">otherness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">segregation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hierarchical" title="Hierarchical">hierarchical</a> ranking, and <a href="/wiki/Supremacism" title="Supremacism">supremacism</a>.
</p><p>While the concepts of race and ethnicity are considered to be separate in contemporary <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social science</a>, the two terms have a long history of equivalence in popular usage and older social science literature. "Ethnicity" is often used in a sense close to one traditionally attributed to "race", the division of human groups based on qualities assumed to be essential or innate to the group (e.g. shared <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestry" title="Ancestry">ancestry</a> or shared behavior). <i>Racism</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Racial_discrimination" title="Racial discrimination">racial discrimination</a></i> are often used to describe discrimination on an ethnic or cultural basis, independent of whether these differences are described as racial. According to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Racial_Discrimination" title="Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination">Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination</a>, there is no distinction between the terms "racial" and "ethnic" discrimination. It further concludes that superiority based on racial differentiation is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientifically_false" title="Scientifically false">scientifically false</a>, morally condemnable, socially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unjust" title="Unjust">unjust</a>, and dangerous. The convention also declared that there is no justification for racial discrimination, anywhere, in theory or in practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Racism is a relatively modern concept, arising in the European <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_imperialism" title="Age of imperialism">age of imperialism</a>, the subsequent growth of <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>, and especially the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dennis_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dennis-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lieberman1997_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lieberman1997-6">[6]</a></sup> of which it was a major driving force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> It was also a major force behind <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation_in_the_United_States" title="Racial segregation in the United States">racial segregation in the United States</a> in the 19th and early 20th centuries, and of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apartheid_in_South_Africa" title="Apartheid in South Africa">apartheid in South Africa</a>; 19th and 20th-century racism in <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a> is particularly well documented and constitutes a reference point in studies and discourses about racism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-8">[8]</a></sup> Racism has played a role in <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_genocide" title="Armenian genocide">Armenian genocide</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">Rwandan genocide</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Genocide_of_Serbs_in_the_Independent_State_of_Croatia" title="Genocide of Serbs in the Independent State of Croatia">Genocide of Serbs in the Independent State of Croatia</a>, as well as colonial projects including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_colonization" title="European colonization">European colonization</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Colonization of the Americas">the Americas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colonisation_of_Africa" title="Colonisation of Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_imperialism_in_Asia" title="Western imperialism in Asia">Asia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Population_transfer_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Population transfer in the Soviet Union">population transfer in the Soviet Union</a> including deportations of indigenous minorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">Indigenous peoples</a> have been—and are—often subject to racist attitudes.
</p>

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Index_of_racism-related_articles" title="Index of racism-related articles">Index</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Category:Race_(human_categorization)" title="Category:Race (human categorization)">Category</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Race" title="Template:Race"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Race" title="Template talk:Race"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Race&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ProceedingsLakeMohonkConference.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="809" data-file-width="1185" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/ProceedingsLakeMohonkConference.png/220px-ProceedingsLakeMohonkConference.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/ProceedingsLakeMohonkConference.png/330px-ProceedingsLakeMohonkConference.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/ProceedingsLakeMohonkConference.png/440px-ProceedingsLakeMohonkConference.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ProceedingsLakeMohonkConference.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early use of the word <i>racism</i> by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Henry_Pratt" title="Richard Henry Pratt">Richard Henry Pratt</a> in 1902: "Association of races and classes is necessary to destroy racism and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classism" title="Classism">classism</a>."</div></div></div>
<p>In the 19th century, many scientists subscribed to the belief that the human population can be divided into races. The term <i>racism</i> is a noun describing the state of being racist, i.e., subscribing to the belief that the human population can or should be classified into races with differential abilities and dispositions, which in turn may motivate a political ideology in which rights and privileges are differentially distributed based on racial categories. The term "racist" may be an adjective or a noun, the latter describing a person who holds those beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merriam-Webster_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merriam-Webster-10">[10]</a></sup> The origin of the root word "race" is not clear. Linguists generally agree that it came to the English language from <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">Middle French</a>, but there is no such agreement on how it generally came into Latin-based languages. A recent proposal is that it derives from the Arabic <i>ra's</i>, which means "head, beginning, origin" or the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> <i>rosh</i>, which has a similar meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Early race theorists generally held the view that some races were inferior to others and they consequently believed that the differential treatment of races was fully justified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garner09_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garner09-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canadian_Encyclo_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canadian_Encyclo-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> These early theories guided <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudo-scientific</a> research assumptions; the collective endeavors to adequately define and form hypotheses about racial differences are generally termed <a href="/wiki/Scientific_racism" title="Scientific racism">scientific racism</a>, though this term is a misnomer, due to the lack of any actual science backing the claims.
</p><p>Most <a href="/wiki/Biologist" title="Biologist">biologists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropologists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociologists</a> reject a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(general)" title="Taxonomy (general)">taxonomy</a> of races in favor of more specific and/or empirically verifiable criteria, such as <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>, ethnicity, or a history of <a href="/wiki/Endogamy" title="Endogamy">endogamy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">Human genome</a> research indicates that race is not a meaningful genetic classification of humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patrinos04_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patrinos04-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keita97_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keita97-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smedley05_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smedley05-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>An entry in the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> (2008) defines <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racialism" title="Racialism">racialism</a> as "[a]n earlier term than racism, but now largely superseded by it", and cites the term "racialism" in a 1902 quote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The revised <i>Oxford English Dictionary</i> cites the shorter term "racism" in a quote from the year 1903.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> It was defined by the <i>Oxford English Dictionary</i> (2nd edition 1989) as "[t]he theory that distinctive human characteristics and abilities are determined by race"; the same dictionary termed <i>racism</i> a <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonym</a> of <i>racialism</i>: "belief in the superiority of a particular race". By the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, <i>racism</i> had acquired the same supremacist connotations formerly associated with <i>racialism</i>: <i>racism</i> by then implied racial <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discrimination</a>, racial <a href="/wiki/Supremacism" title="Supremacism">supremacism</a>, and a harmful intent. The term "race hatred" had also been used by sociologist <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Hertz" title="Frederick Hertz">Frederick Hertz</a> in the late 1920s.
</p><p>As its history indicates, the popular use of the word <i>racism</i> is relatively recent. The word came into widespread usage in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a> in the 1930s, when it was used to describe the social and political ideology of <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a>, which treated "race" as a naturally given political unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> It is commonly agreed that racism existed before the coinage of the word, but there is not a wide agreement on a single definition of what racism is and what it is not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garner09_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Garner09-12">[12]</a></sup> Today, some scholars of racism prefer to use the concept in the plural <i>racisms</i>, in order to emphasize its many different forms that do not easily fall under a single definition. They also argue that different forms of racism have characterized different historical periods and geographical areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bethen14_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bethen14-23">[23]</a></sup> Garner (2009: p. 11) summarizes different existing definitions of racism and identifies three common elements contained in those definitions of racism. First, a historical, hierarchical <a href="/wiki/Power_(social_and_political)" title="Power (social and political)">power</a> relationship between groups; second, a set of ideas (an ideology) about racial differences; and, third, discriminatory actions (practices).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garner09_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Garner09-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal">Legal</span></h3>
<p>Though many countries around the globe have passed <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">laws</a> related to race and discrimination, the first significant international <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> instrument developed by the United Nations (UN) was the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> (UDHR),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNUDHR_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNUDHR-24">[24]</a></sup> which was adopted by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> in 1948. The UDHR recognizes that if people are to be treated with dignity, they require <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_rights" title="Economic rights">economic rights</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights">social rights</a> including education, and the rights to <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultural</a> and political participation and <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberty</a>. It further states that everyone is entitled to these rights "without distinction of any kind, such as race, <a href="/wiki/Human_skin_color" title="Human skin color">colour</a>, sex, language, religion, political or other opinion, <a href="/wiki/Nationality" title="Nationality">national</a> or <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">social</a> origin, property, birth or other status".
</p><p>The UN does not define "racism"; however, it does define "racial discrimination". According to the 1965 UN <a href="/wiki/International_Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Racial_Discrimination" title="International Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination">International Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNICERD_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNICERD-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The term "racial discrimination" shall mean any distinction, exclusion, restriction, or preference based on race, colour, <a href="/wiki/Cultural_heritage" title="Cultural heritage">descent</a>, or national or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnicity" title="Ethnicity">ethnic</a> origin that has the purpose or effect of nullifying or impairing the recognition, enjoyment or exercise, on an equal footing, of human rights and fundamental freedoms in the political, economic, social, cultural or any other field of public life.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In their 1978 <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">United Nations Educational, Scientific, and Cultural Organization (UNESCO)</a> Declaration on Race and Racial Prejudice (Article 1), the UN states, "All human beings belong to a single species and are descended from a common stock. They are born equal in dignity and rights and all form an integral part of humanity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESCO78_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO78-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The UN definition of racial discrimination does not make any distinction between <a href="/wiki/Discrimination_based_on_skin_color" title="Discrimination based on skin color">discrimination based on ethnicity and race</a>, in part because the distinction between the two has been a matter of debate among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academia" title="Academia">academics</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropologists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Similarly, in <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Law of the United Kingdom">British law</a>, the phrase <i>racial group</i> means "any group of people who are defined by reference to their race, colour, nationality (including citizenship) or ethnic or national origin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Norway, the word "race" has been removed from national laws concerning discrimination because the use of the phrase is considered problematic and unethical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The Norwegian Anti-Discrimination Act bans discrimination based on ethnicity, national origin, descent, and skin color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_behavioral_sciences">Social and behavioral sciences</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sociology_of_race_and_ethnic_relations" title="Sociology of race and ethnic relations">Sociology of race and ethnic relations</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">Sociologists</a>, in general, recognize "race" as a <a href="/wiki/Social_constructivism" title="Social constructivism">social construct</a>. This means that, although the concepts of race and racism are based on observable biological characteristics, any conclusions drawn about race on the basis of those observations are heavily influenced by cultural ideologies. Racism, as an ideology, exists in a society at both the individual and institutional level.
</p><p>While much of the research and work on racism during the last half-century or so has concentrated on "white racism" in the Western world, historical accounts of race-based social practices can be found across the globe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gossett_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gossett-32">[32]</a></sup> Thus, racism can be broadly defined to encompass individual and group prejudices and acts of discrimination that result in material and cultural advantages conferred on a majority or a dominant social group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> So-called "white racism" focuses on societies in which white populations are the majority or the dominant social group. In studies of these majority white societies, the aggregate of material and cultural advantages is usually termed "<a href="/wiki/White_privilege" title="White privilege">white privilege</a>".
</p><p>Race and race relations are prominent areas of study in <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>. Much of the sociological literature focuses on white racism. Some of the earliest sociological works on racism were penned by sociologist <a href="/wiki/W._E._B._Du_Bois" title="W. E. B. Du Bois">W. E. B. Du Bois</a>, the first African American to earn a doctoral degree from <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>. Du Bois wrote, "[t]he problem of the twentieth century is the problem of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Color_line_(civil_rights_issue)" title="Color line (civil rights issue)">color line</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DuBois03_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-DuBois03-34">[34]</a></sup> Wellman (1993) defines racism as "culturally sanctioned beliefs, which, regardless of intentions involved, defend the advantages whites have because of the subordinated position of racial minorities".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wellman_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wellman-35">[35]</a></sup> In both sociology and economics, the outcomes of racist actions are often measured by the <a href="/wiki/Social_inequality" title="Social inequality">inequality</a> in <a href="/wiki/Income" title="Income">income</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wealth" title="Wealth">wealth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Net_worth" title="Net worth">net worth</a>, and access to other cultural resources (such as education), between racial groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Massey89_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Massey89-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>In sociology and <a href="/wiki/Social_psychology" title="Social psychology">social psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Identity_(social_science)" title="Identity (social science)">racial identity</a> and the acquisition of that identity, is often used as a variable in racism studies. Racial ideologies and racial identity affect individuals' perception of race and discrimination. Cazenave and Maddern (1999) define racism as "a highly organized system of 'race'-based group privilege that operates at every level of society and is held together by a sophisticated ideology of color/'race' supremacy. Racial centrality (the extent to which a culture recognizes individuals' racial identity) appears to affect the degree of discrimination African-American young adults perceive whereas racial ideology may buffer the detrimental emotional effects of that discrimination."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cazenave_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cazenave-37">[37]</a></sup> Sellers and Shelton (2003) found that a relationship between racial discrimination and emotional distress was moderated by racial ideology and social beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sellers03_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sellers03-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some sociologists also argue that, particularly in the West, where racism is often <a href="/wiki/Social_control" title="Social control">negatively sanctioned</a> in society, racism has changed from being a blatant to a more covert expression of racial prejudice. The "newer" (more hidden and less easily detectable) forms of racism—which can be considered embedded in social processes and structures—are more difficult to explore and challenge. It has been suggested that, while in many countries overt or explicit racism has become increasingly <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboo</a>, even among those who display egalitarian explicit attitudes, an <a href="/wiki/Implicit-association_test" title="Implicit-association test">implicit</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aversive_racism" title="Aversive racism">aversive racism</a> is still maintained subconsciously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwald_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwald-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>This process has been studied extensively in social psychology as implicit associations and <a href="/wiki/Implicit_attitude" title="Implicit attitude">implicit attitudes</a>, a component of <a href="/wiki/Implicit_cognition" title="Implicit cognition">implicit cognition</a>. Implicit attitudes are evaluations that occur without conscious awareness towards an attitude object or the self. These evaluations are generally either favorable or unfavorable. They come about from various influences in the individual experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Implicit attitudes are not consciously identified (or they are inaccurately identified) traces of past experience that mediate favorable or unfavorable feelings, thoughts, or actions towards social objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwald_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwald-39">[39]</a></sup> These feelings, thoughts, or actions have an influence on behavior of which the individual may not be aware.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Therefore, subconscious racism can influence our visual processing and how our minds work when we are subliminally exposed to faces of different colors. In thinking about crime, for example, <a href="/wiki/Social_psychology" title="Social psychology">social psychologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Eberhardt" title="Jennifer Eberhardt">Jennifer L. Eberhardt</a> (2004) of <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> holds that, "blackness is so associated with crime you're ready to pick out these crime objects."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eberhardt04_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eberhardt04-42">[42]</a></sup> Such exposures influence our minds and they can cause subconscious racism in our behavior towards other people or even towards objects. Thus, racist thoughts and actions can arise from stereotypes and fears of which we are not aware.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eberhardt14_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eberhardt14-43">[43]</a></sup> For example, scientists and activists have warned that the use of the stereotype "Nigerian Prince" for referring to <a href="/wiki/Advance-fee_scam" title="Advance-fee scam">advance-fee scammers</a> is racist, i.e. "reducing Nigeria to a nation of scammers and fraudulent princes, as some people still do online, is a <a href="/wiki/Stereotype" title="Stereotype">stereotype</a> that needs to be called out".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Humanities">Humanities</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">Language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">discourse</a> are active areas of study in the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a>, along with literature and the arts. <a href="/wiki/Discourse_analysis" title="Discourse analysis">Discourse analysis</a> seeks to reveal the meaning of race and the actions of racists through careful study of the ways in which these factors of human society are described and discussed in various written and oral works. For example, Van Dijk (1992) examines the different ways in which descriptions of racism and racist actions are depicted by the perpetrators of such actions as well as by their victims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> He notes that when descriptions of actions have negative implications for the majority, and especially for white elites, they are often seen as controversial and such controversial interpretations are typically marked with quotation marks or they are greeted with expressions of distance or doubt. The previously cited book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Souls_of_Black_Folk" title="The Souls of Black Folk">The Souls of Black Folk</a></i> by W.E.B. Du Bois, represents early <a href="/wiki/African-American_literature" title="African-American literature">African-American literature</a> that describes the author's experiences with racism when he was traveling in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">South</a> as an African American.
</p><p>Much American fictional literature has focused on issues of racism and the black "racial experience" in the US, including works written by whites, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Uncle_Tom%27s_Cabin" title="Uncle Tom's Cabin">Uncle Tom's Cabin</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/To_Kill_a_Mockingbird" title="To Kill a Mockingbird">To Kill a Mockingbird</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Imitation_of_Life_(novel)" title="Imitation of Life (novel)">Imitation of Life</a></i>, or even the non-fiction work <i><a href="/wiki/Black_Like_Me" title="Black Like Me">Black Like Me</a></i>. These books, and others like them, feed into what has been called the "<a href="/wiki/White_savior_narrative_in_film" title="White savior narrative in film">white savior narrative in film</a>", in which the heroes and heroines are white even though the story is about things that happen to black characters. <a href="/wiki/Content_analysis" title="Content analysis">Textual analysis</a> of such writings can contrast sharply with black authors' descriptions of African Americans and their experiences in US society. African-American writers have sometimes been portrayed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-American_studies" title="African-American studies">African-American studies</a> as retreating from racial issues when they write about "<a href="/wiki/Whiteness_studies" title="Whiteness studies">whiteness</a>", while others identify this as an African-American literary tradition called "the literature of white estrangement", part of a multi-pronged effort to challenge and dismantle <a href="/wiki/White_supremacy" title="White supremacy">white supremacy</a> in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_usage">Popular usage</span></h3>
<p>According to dictionaries, the word is commonly used to describe prejudice and discrimination based on race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Racism can also be said to describe a condition in society in which a dominant racial group benefits from the <a href="/wiki/Oppression" title="Oppression">oppression</a> of others, whether that group wants such benefits or not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Foucauldian scholar Ladelle McWhorter, in her 2009 book, <i>Racism and Sexual Oppression in Anglo-America: A Genealogy</i>, posits modern racism similarly, focusing on the notion of a dominant group, usually whites, vying for racial purity and progress, rather than an overt or obvious ideology focused on the oppression of nonwhites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In popular usage, as in some academic usage, little distinction is made between "racism" and "<a href="/wiki/Ethnocentrism" title="Ethnocentrism">ethnocentrism</a>". Often, the two are listed together as "racial and ethnic" in describing some action or outcome that is associated with prejudice within a majority or dominant group in society. Furthermore, the meaning of the term racism is often conflated with the terms prejudice, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bigotry" title="Bigotry">bigotry</a>, and discrimination. Racism is a complex concept that can involve each of those; but it cannot be equated with, nor is it synonymous, with these other terms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The term is often used in relation to what is seen as prejudice within a minority or subjugated group, as in the concept of <a href="/wiki/Reverse_racism" title="Reverse racism">reverse racism</a>. "Reverse racism" is a concept often used to describe acts of discrimination or hostility against members of a dominant racial or ethnic group while favoring members of minority groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cashmore_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cashmore-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yee_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yee-52">[52]</a></sup> This concept has been used especially in the United States in debates over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Color-conscious" title="Color-conscious">color-conscious</a> policies (such as <a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action" title="Affirmative action">affirmative action</a>) intended to remedy racial inequalities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> However, many experts and other commenters view reverse racism as a myth rather than a reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Scholars commonly define racism not only in terms of individual prejudice, but also in terms of a power structure that protects the interests of the dominant culture and actively discriminates against ethnic minorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cashmore_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cashmore-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yee_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yee-52">[52]</a></sup> From this perspective, while members of ethnic minorities may be prejudiced against members of the dominant culture, they lack the political and economic power to actively oppress them, and they are therefore not practicing "racism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dennis_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dennis-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cashmore_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cashmore-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aspects">Aspects</span></h2>

<p>The ideology underlying racism can manifest in many aspects of social life. Such aspects are described in this section, although the list is not exhaustive.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aversive_racism">Aversive racism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aversive_racism" title="Aversive racism">Aversive racism</a></div>
<p>Aversive racism is a form of implicit racism, in which a person's unconscious negative evaluations of racial or ethnic minorities are realized by a persistent avoidance of interaction with other racial and ethnic groups. As opposed to traditional, overt racism, which is characterized by overt hatred for and explicit discrimination against racial/ethnic minorities, aversive racism is characterized by more complex, <a href="/wiki/Ambivalence" title="Ambivalence">ambivalent</a> expressions and attitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaertner_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaertner-59">[59]</a></sup> Aversive racism is similar in implications to the concept of symbolic or modern racism (described below), which is also a form of implicit, unconscious, or covert attitude which results in unconscious forms of discrimination.
</p><p>The term was coined by Joel Kovel to describe the subtle racial behaviors of any ethnic or racial group who rationalize their aversion to a particular group by appeal to rules or stereotypes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaertner_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gaertner-59">[59]</a></sup> People who behave in an aversively racial way may profess egalitarian beliefs, and will often deny their racially motivated behavior; nevertheless they change their behavior when dealing with a member of another race or ethnic group than the one they belong to. The motivation for the change is thought to be implicit or subconscious. Experiments have provided empirical support for the existence of aversive racism. Aversive racism has been shown to have potentially serious implications for decision making in employment, in legal decisions and in helping behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Color_blindness">Color blindness</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Color_blindness_(race)" title="Color blindness (race)">Color blindness (race)</a></div>
<p>In relation to racism, color blindness is the disregard of racial characteristics in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_interaction" title="Social interaction">social interaction</a>, for example in the rejection of affirmative action, as a way to address the results of past patterns of discrimination. Critics of this attitude argue that by refusing to attend to racial disparities, racial color blindness in fact unconsciously perpetuates the patterns that produce racial inequality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ansell_2008_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ansell_2008-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eduardo_Bonilla-Silva" title="Eduardo Bonilla-Silva">Eduardo Bonilla-Silva</a> argues that color blind racism arises from an "abstract <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a>, biologization of culture, naturalization of racial matters, and minimization of racism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonilla-Silva_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bonilla-Silva-63">[63]</a></sup> Color blind practices are "subtle, <a href="/wiki/Institution" title="Institution">institutional</a>, and apparently nonracial"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonilla-Silva1_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bonilla-Silva1-64">[64]</a></sup> because race is explicitly ignored in decision-making. If race is disregarded in predominantly white populations, for example, whiteness becomes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norm_(social)" title="Norm (social)">normative</a> standard, whereas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People_of_color" title="People of color">people of color</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Othering" title="Othering">othered</a>, and the racism these individuals experience may be minimized or erased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parker-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Social_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Social-66">[66]</a></sup> At an individual level, people with "color blind prejudice" reject racist ideology, but also reject systemic policies intended to fix <a href="/wiki/Institutional_racism" title="Institutional racism">institutional racism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Social_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Social-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural">Cultural</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_racism" title="Cultural racism">Cultural racism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia" title="Xenophobia">Xenophobia</a></div>
<p>Cultural racism manifests as societal beliefs and customs that promote the assumption that the products of a given culture, including the language and traditions of that culture, are superior to those of other cultures. It shares a great deal with <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia" title="Xenophobia">xenophobia</a>, which is often characterized by fear of, or aggression toward, members of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ingroups_and_outgroups" title="Ingroups and outgroups">outgroup</a> by members of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ingroups_and_outgroups" title="Ingroups and outgroups">ingroup</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="are these definitions from the same Wren article, or maybe from Blaut's? (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
In that sense it is also similar to <a href="/wiki/Communalism_(South_Asia)" title="Communalism (South Asia)">communalism</a> as used in South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cultural racism exists when there is a widespread acceptance of stereotypes concerning diverse ethnic or population groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Whereas racism can be characterised by the belief that one race is inherently superior to another, cultural racism can be characterised by the belief that one culture is inherently superior to another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic">Economic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racial_wage_gap_in_the_United_States" title="Racial wage gap in the United States">Racial wage gap in the United States</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racial_wealth_gap_in_the_United_States" title="Racial wealth gap in the United States">Racial wealth gap in the United States</a></div>
<p>Historical economic or social disparity is alleged to be a form of <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discrimination</a> caused by past racism and historical reasons, affecting the present generation through deficits in the formal education and kinds of preparation in previous generations, and through primarily unconscious racist attitudes and actions on members of the general population. <a href="/wiki/Economic_discrimination" title="Economic discrimination">Economic discrimination</a> may lead to choices that perpetuate racism. For example, color photographic film was tuned for white skin<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> as are automatic soap dispensers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Facial_recognition_system" title="Facial recognition system">facial recognition systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America" title="Bank of America">Bank of America</a> agreed to pay $335 million to settle a federal government claim that its mortgage division, Countrywide Financial, <a href="/wiki/Mortgage_discrimination" title="Mortgage discrimination">discriminated</a> against black and Hispanic homebuyers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-america_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-america-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutional">Institutional</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vivian_Malone_registering.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3014" data-file-width="4642" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Vivian_Malone_registering.jpg/300px-Vivian_Malone_registering.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Vivian_Malone_registering.jpg/450px-Vivian_Malone_registering.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Vivian_Malone_registering.jpg/600px-Vivian_Malone_registering.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vivian_Malone_registering.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African-American</a> university student <a href="/wiki/Vivian_Malone_Jones" title="Vivian Malone Jones">Vivian Malone</a> entering the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama" title="University of Alabama">University of Alabama</a> in the U.S. to register for classes as one of the first African-American students to attend the institution. Until 1963, the university was <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">racially segregated</a> and African-American students were not allowed to attend.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Institutional_racism" title="Institutional racism">Institutional racism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_racism" title="State racism">State racism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Racial_profiling" title="Racial profiling">Racial profiling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Racism_by_country" title="Racism by country">Racism by country</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Institutional_racism" title="Institutional racism">Institutional racism</a> (also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structural_racism" title="Structural racism">structural racism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_racism" title="State racism">state racism</a> or systemic racism) is racial discrimination by governments, corporations, religions, or educational institutions or other large organizations with the power to influence the lives of many individuals. <a href="/wiki/Stokely_Carmichael" title="Stokely Carmichael">Stokely Carmichael</a> is credited for coining the phrase <i>institutional racism</i> in the late 1960s. He defined the term as "the collective failure of an organization to provide an appropriate and professional service to people because of their colour, culture or ethnic origin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Maulana_Karenga" title="Maulana Karenga">Maulana Karenga</a> argued that racism constituted the destruction of culture, language, religion, and human possibility and that the effects of racism were "the morally monstrous destruction of human possibility involved redefining African humanity to the world, poisoning past, present and future relations with others who only know us through this stereotyping and thus damaging the truly human relations among peoples".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Othering">Othering</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Othering" title="Othering">Othering</a></div>
<p>Othering is the term used by some to describe a system of discrimination whereby the characteristics of a group are used to distinguish them as separate from the norm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Othering plays a fundamental role in the history and continuation of racism. To <a href="/wiki/Objectification" title="Objectification">objectify</a> a culture as something different, exotic or underdeveloped is to generalize that it is not like 'normal' society. Europe's colonial attitude towards the Orientals exemplifies this as it was thought that the East was the opposite of the West; feminine where the West was masculine, weak where the West was strong and traditional where the West was progressive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Said,_Edward_1978_pp.357_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Said,_Edward_1978_pp.357-77">[77]</a></sup> By making these generalizations and othering the East, Europe was simultaneously defining herself as the norm, further entrenching the gap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Much of the process of othering relies on imagined difference, or the expectation of difference. Spatial difference can be enough to conclude that "we" are "here" and the "others" are over "there".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Said,_Edward_1978_pp.357_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Said,_Edward_1978_pp.357-77">[77]</a></sup> Imagined differences serve to categorize people into groups and assign them characteristics that suit the imaginer's expectations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Racial_discrimination">Racial discrimination</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Racial_discrimination" title="Racial discrimination">Racial discrimination</a></div>
<p>Racial discrimination refers to <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discrimination</a> against someone on the basis of their race.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Racial_segregation">Racial segregation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">Racial segregation</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox" style="width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:115%">External video</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><img alt="video icon" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=N6LxuFpDIYM" rel="nofollow">James A. White Sr.: The little problem I had renting a house</a>, <a href="/wiki/TED_(conference)" title="TED (conference)">TED Talks</a>, 14:20, February 20, 2015</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Racial segregation is the separation of humans into <a href="/wiki/Race_(human_categorization)" title="Race (human categorization)">socially-constructed racial groups</a> in daily life. It may apply to activities such as eating in a restaurant, drinking from a water fountain, using a bathroom, attending school, going to the movies, or in the rental or purchase of a home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Segregation is generally outlawed, but may exist through social norms, even when there is no strong individual preference for it, as suggested by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Schelling" title="Thomas Schelling">Thomas Schelling</a>'s models of segregation and subsequent work.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supremacism">Supremacism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supremacism" title="Supremacism">Supremacism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1899BalanceCartoon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="622" data-file-width="428" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/1899BalanceCartoon.jpg/220px-1899BalanceCartoon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/1899BalanceCartoon.jpg/330px-1899BalanceCartoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/46/1899BalanceCartoon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1899BalanceCartoon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1899 <a href="/wiki/Uncle_Sam" title="Uncle Sam">Uncle Sam</a> (a personification of the United States) balances his new possessions which are depicted as savage children. The figures are Puerto Rico, Hawaii, Cuba, Philippines and "Ladrones" (the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Islands" title="Mariana Islands">Mariana Islands</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>Centuries of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_colonialism" title="European colonialism">European colonialism</a> in the Americas, Africa and Asia were often justified by <a href="/wiki/White_supremacy" title="White supremacy">white supremacist</a> attitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> During the early 20th century, the phrase "<a href="/wiki/The_White_Man%27s_Burden" title="The White Man's Burden">The White Man's Burden</a>" was widely used to justify an <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialist</a> policy as a noble enterprise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> A justification for the policy of conquest and subjugation of <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> emanated from the stereotyped perceptions of the indigenous people as "merciless Indian savages", as they are described in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Sam Wolfson of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> writes that "the declaration's passage has often been cited as an encapsulation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dehumanizing" title="Dehumanizing">dehumanizing</a> attitude toward indigenous Americans that the US was founded on."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In an 1890 article about colonial expansion onto Native American land, author <a href="/wiki/L._Frank_Baum" title="L. Frank Baum">L. Frank Baum</a> wrote: "The Whites, by law of conquest, by justice of civilization, are masters of the American continent, and the best safety of the frontier settlements will be secured by the total annihilation of the few remaining Indians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Attitudes of <a href="/wiki/Black_supremacy" title="Black supremacy">black supremacy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_supremacy" title="Arab supremacy">Arab supremacy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sinocentrism" title="Sinocentrism">East Asian supremacy</a> also exist.
</p>
<h3><span id="Symbolic.2Fmodern"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolic/modern">Symbolic/modern</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Symbolic_racism" title="Symbolic racism">Symbolic racism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Little_Rock_integration_protest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="995" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Little_Rock_integration_protest.jpg/220px-Little_Rock_integration_protest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Little_Rock_integration_protest.jpg/330px-Little_Rock_integration_protest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Little_Rock_integration_protest.jpg/440px-Little_Rock_integration_protest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Little_Rock_integration_protest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A rally against <a href="/wiki/Desegregation_busing" title="Desegregation busing">school integration</a> in <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock,_Arkansas" title="Little Rock, Arkansas">Little Rock, Arkansas</a>, 1959</div></div></div>
<p>Some scholars argue that in the US, earlier violent and aggressive forms of racism have evolved into a more subtle form of prejudice in the late 20th century. This new form of racism is sometimes referred to as "modern racism" and it is characterized by outwardly acting unprejudiced while inwardly maintaining prejudiced attitudes, displaying subtle prejudiced behaviors such as actions informed by attributing qualities to others based on racial stereotypes, and evaluating the same behavior differently based on the race of the person being evaluated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> This view is based on studies of prejudice and discriminatory behavior, where some people will act ambivalently towards black people, with positive reactions in certain, more public contexts, but more negative views and expressions in more private contexts. This ambivalence may also be visible for example in hiring decisions where job candidates that are otherwise positively evaluated may be unconsciously disfavored by employers in the final decision because of their race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Some scholars consider modern racism to be characterized by an explicit rejection of stereotypes, combined with resistance to changing structures of discrimination for reasons that are ostensibly non-racial, an ideology that considers opportunity at a purely individual basis denying the relevance of race in determining individual opportunities and the exhibition of indirect forms of micro-aggression toward and/or avoidance of people of other races.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subconscious_biases">Subconscious biases</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Implicit_bias#Racial_bias" title="Implicit bias">Implicit bias § Racial bias</a></div>
<p>Recent research has shown that individuals who consciously claim to reject racism may still exhibit race-based subconscious biases in their decision-making processes. While such "subconscious racial biases" do not fully fit the definition of racism, their impact can be similar, though typically less pronounced, not being explicit, conscious or deliberate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_law_and_racial_discrimination">International law and racial discrimination</span></h2>
<p>In 1919, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racial_Equality_Proposal,_1919" title="Racial Equality Proposal, 1919">proposal</a> to include a racial equality provision in the <a href="/wiki/Covenant_of_the_League_of_Nations" title="Covenant of the League of Nations">Covenant of the League of Nations</a> was supported by a majority, but not adopted in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference</a> in 1919. In 1943, Japan and its allies declared work for the abolition of racial discrimination to be their aim at the <a href="/wiki/Greater_East_Asia_Conference" title="Greater East Asia Conference">Greater East Asia Conference</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Article 1 of the 1945 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_Charter" title="UN Charter">UN Charter</a> includes "promoting and encouraging respect for human rights and for fundamental freedoms for all without distinction as to race" as UN purpose.
</p><p>In 1950, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> suggested in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Race_Question" title="The Race Question">The Race Question</a></i>—a statement signed by 21 scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Ashley_Montagu" title="Ashley Montagu">Ashley Montagu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claude_L%C3%A9vi-Strauss" title="Claude Lévi-Strauss">Claude Lévi-Strauss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gunnar_Myrdal" title="Gunnar Myrdal">Gunnar Myrdal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julian_Huxley" title="Julian Huxley">Julian Huxley</a>, etc.—to "drop the term <i>race</i> altogether and instead speak of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups" title="Ethnic groups">ethnic groups</a>". The statement condemned <a href="/wiki/Scientific_racism" title="Scientific racism">scientific racism</a> theories that had played a role in <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a>. It aimed both at debunking scientific racist theories, by popularizing modern knowledge concerning "the race question", and morally condemned racism as contrary to the philosophy of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> and its assumption of <a href="/wiki/Social_equality" title="Social equality">equal rights</a> for all. Along with Myrdal's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/An_American_Dilemma:_The_Negro_Problem_and_Modern_Democracy" title="An American Dilemma: The Negro Problem and Modern Democracy">An American Dilemma: The Negro Problem and Modern Democracy</a></i> (1944), <i>The Race Question</i> influenced the 1954 U.S. Supreme Court <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation_in_the_United_States" title="Racial segregation in the United States">desegregation</a> decision in <i><a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Board_of_Education" title="Brown v. Board of Education">Brown v. Board of Education</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prins_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prins-94">[94]</a></sup> Also, in 1950, the <a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="European Convention on Human Rights">European Convention on Human Rights</a> was adopted, which was widely used on racial discrimination issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United Nations use the definition of racial discrimination laid out in the <i><a href="/wiki/International_Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Racial_Discrimination" title="International Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination">International Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination</a></i>, adopted in 1966:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>... any distinction, exclusion, restriction or preference based on race, color, descent, or national or ethnic origin that has the purpose or effect of nullifying or impairing the recognition, enjoyment or exercise, on an equal footing, of human rights and fundamental freedoms in the political, economic, social, cultural or any other field of public life. (Part 1 of Article 1 of the U.N. International Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination)
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 2001, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> explicitly banned racism, along with many other forms of social discrimination, in the <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_Fundamental_Rights_of_the_European_Union" title="Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union">Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union</a>, the legal effect of which, if any, would necessarily be limited to <a href="/wiki/Institutions_of_the_European_Union" title="Institutions of the European Union">Institutions of the European Union</a>: "Article 21 of the charter prohibits discrimination on any ground such as race, color, ethnic or social origin, genetic features, language, religion or belief, political or any other opinion, membership of a national minority, property, disability, age or sexual orientation and also discrimination on the grounds of nationality."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideology">Ideology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Racistcampaignposter1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1036" data-file-width="1290" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Racistcampaignposter1.jpg/220px-Racistcampaignposter1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Racistcampaignposter1.jpg/330px-Racistcampaignposter1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Racistcampaignposter1.jpg/440px-Racistcampaignposter1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Racistcampaignposter1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pro–<a href="/wiki/Hiester_Clymer" title="Hiester Clymer">Hiester Clymer</a> racist political campaign poster from the <a href="/wiki/1866_Pennsylvania_gubernatorial_election" title="1866 Pennsylvania gubernatorial election">1866 Pennsylvania gubernatorial election</a></div></div></div>
<p>Racism existed during the 19th century as "<a href="/wiki/Scientific_racism" title="Scientific racism">scientific racism</a>", which attempted to provide a <a href="/wiki/Typology_(anthropology)" title="Typology (anthropology)">racial classification</a> of humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> In 1775 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_Blumenbach" title="Johann Blumenbach">Johann Blumenbach</a> divided the world's population into five groups according to skin color (Caucasians, Mongols, etc.), positing the view that the non-Caucasians had arisen through a process of degeneration. Another early view in scientific racism was the <a href="/wiki/Polygenism" title="Polygenism">polygenist view</a>, which held that the different races had been separately created. Polygenist <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Meiners" title="Christoph Meiners">Christoph Meiners</a> for example, split mankind into two divisions which he labeled the "beautiful White race" and the "ugly Black race". In Meiners' book, <i>The Outline of History of Mankind</i>, he claimed that a main characteristic of race is either beauty or ugliness. He viewed only the white race as beautiful. He considered ugly races to be inferior, immoral and animal-like.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anders_Retzius" title="Anders Retzius">Anders Retzius</a> demonstrated that neither Europeans nor others are one "pure race", but of mixed origins. While <a href="/wiki/Typology_(anthropology)#Typology" title="Typology (anthropology)">discredited</a>, derivations of Blumenbach's taxonomy are <a href="/wiki/Definitions_of_whiteness_in_the_United_States" title="Definitions of whiteness in the United States">still widely used</a> for the classification of the population in the United States. <a href="/wiki/Hans_Peder_Steensby" title="Hans Peder Steensby">Hans Peder Steensby</a>, while strongly emphasizing that all humans today are of mixed origins, in 1907 claimed that the origins of human differences must be traced extraordinarily far back in time, and conjectured that the "purest race" today would be the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_Aboriginals" title="Australian Aboriginals">Australian Aboriginals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DurbanSign1989.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="587" decoding="async" height="375" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/DurbanSign1989.jpg/220px-DurbanSign1989.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/DurbanSign1989.jpg/330px-DurbanSign1989.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/DurbanSign1989.jpg/440px-DurbanSign1989.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DurbanSign1989.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sign on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racially_segregated" title="Racially segregated">racially segregated</a> beach during the <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">Apartheid</a> era in South Africa, stating that the area is for the "sole use of members of the white race group"</div></div></div>
<p>Scientific racism fell strongly out of favor in the early 20th century, but the origins of fundamental human and societal differences are still researched within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academia" title="Academia">academia</a>, in fields such as <a href="/wiki/Human_genetics" title="Human genetics">human genetics</a> including <a href="/wiki/Paleogenetics" title="Paleogenetics">paleogenetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_anthropology" title="Social anthropology">social anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comparative_politics" title="Comparative politics">comparative politics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_religions" title="History of religions">history of religions</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_ideas" title="History of ideas">history of ideas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a>, <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatry</a>. There is widespread rejection of any methodology based on anything similar to Blumenbach's races. It is more unclear to which extent and when <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_stereotype" title="Ethnic stereotype">ethnic</a> and national <a href="/wiki/Archetype" title="Archetype">stereotypes</a> are accepted.
</p><p>Although after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a>, racist ideologies were discredited on ethical, political and scientific grounds, racism and <a href="/wiki/Racial_discrimination" title="Racial discrimination">racial discrimination</a> have remained widespread around the world.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W.E.B._Du_Bois" title="W.E.B. Du Bois">Du Bois</a> observed that it is not so much "race" that we think about, but culture: "... a common history, common laws and religion, similar habits of thought and a conscious striving together for certain ideals of life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Late 19th century nationalists were the first to embrace contemporary discourses on "race", ethnicity, and "<a href="/wiki/Survival_of_the_fittest" title="Survival of the fittest">survival of the fittest</a>" to shape new nationalist doctrines. Ultimately, race came to represent not only the most important traits of the human body, but was also regarded as decisively shaping the character and personality of the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> According to this view, culture is the physical manifestation created by ethnic groupings, as such fully determined by racial characteristics. Culture and race became considered intertwined and dependent upon each other, sometimes even to the extent of including nationality or language to the set of definition. Pureness of race tended to be related to rather superficial characteristics that were easily addressed and advertised, such as blondness. Racial qualities tended to be related to nationality and language rather than the actual geographic distribution of racial characteristics. In the case of <a href="/wiki/Nordicism" title="Nordicism">Nordicism</a>, the denomination "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_people" title="Germanic people">Germanic</a>" was equivalent to superiority of race.
</p><p>Bolstered by some <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethnocentrism" title="Ethnocentrism">ethnocentric</a> values and achievements of choice, this concept of racial superiority evolved to distinguish from other cultures that were considered inferior or impure. This emphasis on culture corresponds to the modern mainstream definition of racism: "[r]acism does not originate from the existence of 'races'. It <i>creates</i> them through a process of social division into categories: anybody can be racialised, independently of their somatic, cultural, religious differences."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>This definition explicitly ignores the biological concept of race, which is still subject to scientific debate. In the words of <a href="/wiki/David_C._Rowe" title="David C. Rowe">David C. Rowe</a>, "[a] racial concept, although sometimes in the guise of another name, will remain in use in biology and in other fields because scientists, as well as lay persons, are fascinated by human diversity, some of which is captured by race."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Racial prejudice became subject to international legislation. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Racial_Discrimination" title="Declaration on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination">Declaration on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination</a>, adopted by the United Nations General Assembly on November 20, 1963, addresses racial prejudice explicitly next to discrimination for reasons of race, colour or ethnic origin (Article I).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnicity_and_ethnic_conflicts">Ethnicity and ethnic conflicts</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnicity" title="Ethnicity">Ethnicity</a></div>
